            <div class="headline-container">
    <h1 class="headline h1">
        Global Carbon Emissions Rise for First Time Since 2014
    </h1>
</div>
        
    
        
            
            

<span class="js-scroll-trigger" data-target="body" data-triggers="render-rebelbar" data-toggle-class="with-floating-shares"></span>
<div id="utm_campaign"></div><div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2551232087"
            
                data-authors="Lorraine Chow"
            
            
                data-headline="Global Carbon Emissions Rise for First Time Since 2014"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><p style="">
	Global carbon dioxide 
	<a href="http://ecowatch.com/tag/greenhouse-gases">emissions</a> from <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/energy-news/">energy</a> increased for the first time in 2017 after three years of remaining flat, the <a href="https://www.iea.org/" target="_blank">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA) said Thursday, meaning the world remains far off course in curbing <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/climate-change">planet warming</a> emissions.
</p><p style="">
	Carbon emissions reached a record-high of 32.5 gigatonnes in 2017 due to global economic growth and increased energy demands that was met mostly by fossil fuels. As the 
	<a href="https://www.ft.com/content/c9454688-2d10-11e8-9b4b-bc4b9f08f381" target="_blank">Financial Times</a> noted, that growth—an increase of 460 million tonnes—is the equivalent to the emissions of an additional 170 million cars.
</p><hr/><p style="">
	<span></span>These findings are part of the Paris-based IEA's "<a href="https://www.iea.org/geco/" target="_blank">Global Energy and CO2 Status Report</a>" released today.
</p><p style="">
	The report shows that global energy demand rose by 2.1 percent last year (or 
	<a href="https://www.reuters.com/article/us-energy-carbon-iea/global-carbon-emissions-hit-record-high-in-2017-idUSKBN1GY0RB" target="_blank">14,050 million tonnes</a> of oil equivalent), more than twice the rate in 2016. Oil, natural gas and coal met more than 70 percent of additional need, while <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/renewable-energy/">renewable energy</a> met just about the rest.
</p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image" style="">
	<img class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="NXNVT61521727826" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image" id="de279" src="https://assets.rbl.ms/17461378/980x.png"/>
	<small class="image-media media-caption" placeholder="add caption...">Global energy-related CO2 emissions grew by 1.4 percent in 2017, reaching a historic high of 32.5 gigatonnes, a resumption of growth after three years of global emissions remaining flat.</small><small class="image-media media-photo-credit" placeholder="add photo credit...">Global Energy &amp; CO2 Status Report</small>
</p><p style="">
	"The significant growth in global energy-related carbon dioxide emissions in 2017 tells us that current efforts to combat climate change are far from sufficient," said Fatih Birol, IEA executive director, in a statement.
</p><p style="">
	"For example, there has been a dramatic slowdown in the rate of improvement in global energy efficiency as policy makers have put less focus in this area."
</p><p style="">
	The IEA's findings "demonstrates that current efforts are insufficient to meet the objectives of the Paris Agreement," the report says. The 2015 
	<a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/paris-agreement">Paris agreement</a> set a primary goal to limit global warming well below 2°C to avoid dangerous climate tipping points.
</p><p style="">
	"Global emissions need to peak soon and decline steeply to 2020; this decline will now need to be even greater given the increase in emissions in 2017," the report says.
</p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-twitter_embed">
	<iframe class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="IEXC8E1521727826" frameborder="0" height="150" id="twitter-embed-930075752615370757" scrolling="no" src="/res/community/twitter_embed/?iframe_id=twitter-embed-930075752615370757&amp;created_ts=1510582314.0&amp;screen_name=EcoWatch&amp;text=Analysis%3A+Global+%23CO2+Emissions+Set+to+Rise+2%25+in+2017+After+Three-Year+%E2%80%98Plateau%E2%80%99+https%3A%2F%2Ft.co%2Fz7frg8txHi%E2%80%A6+https%3A%2F%2Ft.co%2FYMNnvsBEBU&amp;id=930075752615370757&amp;name=EcoWatch" width="100%"></iframe>
</p><p style="">
	<span></span>However, this increase was not universal. While most major economies saw a rise, others such as the U.S., UK, Mexico and Japan experienced declines in CO2 emissions.
</p><p style="">
	Interestingly, the greatest decline came from the U.S., where emissions fell by 0.5 percent, the third consecutive year of decline. This is despite 
	<a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/trump-watch/">President Trump</a>'s push for fossil fuels and his intention to withdraw from the Paris agreement.
</p><p style="">
	The reason behind the drop? Mainly because of higher deployment of renewables. In the U.S., a record 
	<a href="https://www.eia.gov/tools/faqs/faq.php?id=427&amp;t=3" target="_blank">17 percent</a> of electricity generation was from renewables last year. According to the report, 10 gigawatts of solar PV was added in 2017, down 30 percent relative to 2016 but still the second highest year on record.
</p><p style="">
	The report also highlighted the clean energy strides of another major global polluter and the world's largest heat consumer: China.
</p><p style="">
	<span></span>"China's economy grew nearly 7 percent last year but emissions increased by just 1.7 percent (or 150 Mt) thanks to continued renewables deployment and faster coal-to-gas switching."
</p><p class="shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image" style="">
	<img class="rm-shortcode" data-rm-shortcode-id="Z37PDV1521727826" data-rm-shortcode-name="rebelmouse-image" id="80a84" src="https://assets.rbl.ms/17461380/980x.png"/>
	<small class="image-media media-caption" placeholder="add caption...">Asian economies accounted for two-thirds of the global increase in carbon emissions.</small><small class="image-media media-photo-credit" placeholder="add photo credit...">Global Energy &amp; CO2 Status Report</small>
</p><p style="">
	Renewable energy played the most important role in the growth of low-carbon energy, the report points out. The sector saw the highest rate of growth of any energy source in 2017, and met around a quarter of global energy demand growth last year.
</p><p style="">
	Here are other 
	<a href="https://www.iea.org/newsroom/news/2018/march/global-energy-demand-grew-by-21-in-2017-and-carbon-emissions-rose-for-the-firs.html" target="_blank">key findings</a> of the IEA report:
</p><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Oil demand grew by 1.6 percent, more than twice the average annual rate seen over the past decade, driven by the transport sector (in particular a growing share of SUVs and trucks in major economies) as well as rising petrochemical demand.</li></ul><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Natural gas consumption grew 3 percent, the most of all fossil fuels, with China alone accounting for nearly a third of this growth, and the buildings and industry sectors contributing to 80 percent of the increase in global demand.</li></ul><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Coal demand rose about 1 percent, reversing declines over the previous two years, driven by an increase in coal-fired electricity generation mostly in Asia.</li></ul><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Renewables had the highest growth rate of any fuel, meeting a quarter of world energy demand growth, as renewables-based electricity generation rose 6.3 percent, driven by expansion of wind, solar and hydropower.</li></ul><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Electricity generation increased by 3.1 percent, significantly faster than overall energy demand, and India and China together accounting for 70 percent of the global increase.</li></ul><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Energy efficiency improvements slowed significantly, with global energy intensity improving by only 1.7 percent in 2017 compared with 2.3 percent on average over the last three years, caused by an apparent slowdown in efficiency policy coverage and stringency and lower energy prices.</li></ul><ul class="ee-ul">
	<li>Fossil fuels accounted for 81 percent of total energy demand in 2017, a level that has remained stable for more than three decades.</li>
</ul>
<div class="around-the-web">
    <div class="around-the-web__headline">Related Articles Around the Web</div>
    <ul class="around-the-web__list">
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="http://www.wri.org/blog/2017/04/interactive-chart-explains-worlds-top-10-emitters-and-how-theyve-changed" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">This Interactive Chart Explains World&#39;s Top 10 Emitters, and How ... &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="http://www.globalcarbonatlas.org/en/CO2-emissions" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">CO2 Emissions | Global Carbon Atlas &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
    </ul>
</div>
</div>
        

        



    </div>



        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    
        
    </div></div></div>
</article>

            <h1 class="widget__headline h1">
    <a class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/earth-hour-2018-wwf-2551416058.html" >
        Skylines to Switch Off as Millions Connect to the Planet to Celebrate Earth Hour 2018
    </a>
</h1>
        
    
        
            
            
        
    
        
            
            
<div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2551416058"
            
                data-authors="World Wildlife Fund"
            
            
                data-headline="Skylines to Switch Off as Millions Connect to the Planet to Celebrate Earth Hour 2018"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief">
                <p>On Saturday, March 24 at 8:30 p.m. local time, skylines around the world will go dark as millions celebrate WWF's Earth Hour to spark global awareness and action on nature and the environment.</p><p>From the Eiffel Tower to the Empire State Building, and the Bird's Nest stadium to Burj Khalifa, thousands of landmarks will switch off their lights in solidarity for the planet, urging individuals, businesses and governments worldwide to move forward the conversations and solutions we need to build a healthy, <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/sustainability">sustainable</a> future for all.</p>
            </div>
            <div class="js-appear-on-expand">
                <script type="keep-reading-content">
                        &lt;p&gt;For the first time, in addition to participating in the global switch-off, WWF is inviting people across the globe to &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.connect2earth.org/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;connect2earth&lt;/a&gt; to share and talk about what nature means to them, in the places they live in and care about. As the planet faces the dual challenge of climate change and the staggering loss of nature, the world&#39;s largest grassroots movement for the environment aims to mobilize unprecedented global action on &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/biodiversity&#34;&gt;biodiversity&lt;/a&gt; and nature by kick-starting global conversations on issues such as healthy &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/forests&#34;&gt;forests&lt;/a&gt;, plastic-free &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/oceans&#34;&gt;oceans&lt;/a&gt; and wildlife &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/conservation&#34;&gt;conservation&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;Nature is in alarming decline. Halting its loss is urgent and crucial as much as tackling &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/climate-change/&#34;&gt;climate change&lt;/a&gt;,&#34; said Marco Lambertini, director general of WWF International. &#34;Biodiversity and nature is the foundation of life, essential to our wellbeing. Yet we continue to take nature for granted while our actions are pushing it to the brink. This Earth Hour, we want to shine a light on the importance of biodiversity and nature. Together, as individuals, businesses and governments, we must show the same determination to halt biodiversity and nature loss as we have shown on climate action to secure a healthy, thriving and living planet for all.&#34; &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the past decade, Earth Hour has inspired millions to support and participate in critical climate and conservation projects led by WWF and many others, helping drive climate policy, awareness and action worldwide. Among its highlights, the movement has helped ban all &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/plastics&#34;&gt;plastics&lt;/a&gt; in the Galapagos in 2014, plant 17 million trees in Kazakhstan, light up homes with solar power in India and the Philippines and push new legislation for the protection of seas and forests in Russia. Today, as biodiversity loss threatens global ambition on climate action and sustainable development, Earth Hour will focus its efforts on building mainstream support for action on biodiversity and nature.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;The totality of forms of life on the planet, what we call biodiversity, provides the infrastructure for the healthy functioning of natural systems,&#34; said Cristiana Paşca Palmer, executive secretary of the &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.cbd.int&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Convention on Biological Diversity&lt;/a&gt;. &#34;This is critical not only to safeguarding life on Earth but also to the wellbeing and development of the human species. Once this natural capital is destroyed, life on Earth, as we know it currently, will no longer be possible. It is therefore imperative to take action, today and every day, to help safeguard biodiversity. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;Earth Hour links people from all around the globe ... It is my great hope that together with our partners from WWF, Earth Hour will inspire and mobilize a wave of actions ... WWF is a dynamic partner of the Secretariat of the UN Convention on Biological Diversity, helping to raise awareness of the importance of biodiversity and nature for people and communities worldwide.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2018, French Polynesia is expected to move to protect 5 million square kilometers (approximately 1.9 million square miles) of its seas to preserve ocean ecosystems. In Kenya, school children will plant 500,000 trees to help reduce &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/deforestation&#34;&gt;deforestation&lt;/a&gt; and in Hong Kong and the UK, people will pledge to shift toward sustainable living. In Colombia, people will call for the country to commit to zero deforestation by 2020. In China and Singapore, WWF will mobilize public support for plastic-free lifestyles.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Recognizing the critical role young people will play in creating a more sustainable world, WWF is also joining forces with the World Organization of the Scout Movement to inspire scouts worldwide to be a part of the global movement—and momentum—tackling the planet&#39;s most pressing environmental challenges and to become environmental leaders of tomorrow.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Starting as a symbolic lights out event in Sydney in 2007, today Earth Hour is celebrated in more than 180 countries and territories across the globe. Earth Hour 2018 will take place on Saturday, March 24 at 8:30 p.m. local time. Visit &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.earthhour.org/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;www.earthhour.org&lt;/a&gt; to know more and read individuals&#39; stories about what they are doing for our planet.&lt;/p&gt;

    <span class="social-date__text">6h</span>

                        <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/ohio-bobcat-trapping-plan-2551408455.html" >
                            
                            <div class="widget__image crop-2x1"
                                
                                    
                                    data-runner-img-hd="https://assets.rbl.ms/17462917/1200x600.jpg"
                                    data-runner-img-md="https://assets.rbl.ms/17462917/600x300.jpg"

    <div class="photo-credit">
        Save Ohio's Bobcat's is working to oppose a proposed trapping season for the recently-threatened feelines.  <em><a href="https://www.facebook.com/saveohiosbobcats/" target="_blank">Save Ohio's Bobcats</a> </em>

            <h1 class="widget__headline h1">
    <a class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/ohio-bobcat-trapping-plan-2551408455.html" >
        Concerned Ohioans Unite Against Bobcat Trapping Plan
    </a>
</h1>
        
    
        
            
            
        
    
        
            
            
<div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2551408455"
            
                data-authors="Olivia Rosane"
            
            
                data-headline="Concerned Ohioans Unite Against Bobcat Trapping Plan"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief">
                <p>Environmental activists, science educators and the Athens Ohio City Council are teaming up against a controversial new proposal by the Ohio Department of Natural Resources Division of Wildlife (ODNRDOW) to open a bobcat trapping season in the southeastern part of the state, <a href="http://thenewpolitical.com/2018/03/21/bobcats-beware-local-activists-fight-back-odnr-plan-allow-bobcat-trapping/" target="_blank">The New Political</a> reported Wednesday. </p>
            </div>
            <div class="js-appear-on-expand">
                <script type="keep-reading-content">
                        &lt;p&gt;The Athens City Council passed a resolution opposing the measure Monday night, citing concerns that bobcats were taken off the state&#39;s threatened and &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/endangered-species&#34; target=&#34;_self&#34;&gt;endangered species&lt;/a&gt; list less than four years ago and that not enough research has been done to determine if the newly-growing populations can sustain a trapping season, according to a copy of the resolution obtained by EcoWatch. The city council also looked to prevent similar proposals in the future, urging &#34;the State of Ohio to declare the bobcat a protected species and ban all hunting and trapping of this wonderful &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/animals/&#34; target=&#34;_self&#34;&gt;animal&lt;/a&gt; for all Ohioans now and in the future.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The resolution notes that bobcats do not overpopulate. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;I like the idea that there&#39;s wild animals in the woods,&#34; Athens City Councilman Jeff Risner told &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.thepostathens.com/article/2018/03/city-council-opposes-bobcat-trapping&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;The Post&lt;/a&gt;. &#34;They&#39;re worth more to me alive than they are dead.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The resolution further notes that the trapping proposal contradicts the ODNRDOW&#39;s own research plans. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In an October 2017 report, the department wrote, &#34;Little is known about the density and distribution of bobcats in Ohio, as well as the population trajectory, and which areas act as source populations. Such information is critical before decisions are taken on opening a trapping season and the maximum yearly take.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The ODNR entered into a contract with the &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ohio-forum.com/2018/02/popescu-lab-takes-4-year-study-bobcats-returning-home-ohio/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Ohio University&lt;/a&gt; in August 2017 to conduct such a study, which will take four years to complete.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;So why the rush and sudden turn-around??,&#34; Heather Cantino, an environmental educator and vice chair of the Buckeye Forest Council board who drafted the council resolution, asked in &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.athensnews.com/opinion/readers_forum/please-oppose-the-fllawed-bobcat-trap-kill-proposal/article_725adb74-27a9-11e8-ae84-6774f8240895.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;The Athens&#39; News Readers Forum&lt;/a&gt;. &#34;Politics and pressure from the Ohio Trappers Association seem to be trumping the DOW&#39;s own recent science-based plan to protect Ohio&#39;s top native predator species,&#34; she wrote.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&#34;http://codes.ohio.gov/oac/1501%253A31-16&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Chapter 1501:31-16&lt;/a&gt; of the Ohio Revised Code values a bobcat at $500. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Bobcats are making a comeback in Ohio—there were 499 verified sightings in 2017, &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.cantonrep.com/news/20180312/bobcat-population-in-ohio-could-be-thinned-by-trapping&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;The Columbia Dispatch&lt;/a&gt; reported. &#34;We&#39;re confident the population is secure,&#34; ODNR wildlife management and research executive administrator Mike Reynolds told The Dispatch. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The proposed trapping season would last from November 2018 to January 2019 and end once a maximum of 40 bobcats are trapped in the east and 20 in the south, where Athens is located, The Dispatch reported. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But Cantino pointed out that each verified sighting does not necessarily correspond to a different animal and that the increase in sightings might be due to the increase in trail cameras installed in the past years, which the ODNRDOW itself suggested in its &lt;a href=&#34;http://wildlife.ohiodnr.gov/Portals/wildlife/PDFs/Species%2520and%2520Habitats/Ohio%2520Bobcat%2520Management%2520Plan.pdf&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Ohio Bobcat Management Plan&lt;/a&gt;. Further, she expressed concerns that the hunting licenses themselves will be unlimited and sell for $5, meaning hunters might go over the proposed quota before the state can enforce it. Finally, not enough research has been done to determine if the quota amounts are really safe for the existing populations. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Assistant professor of conservation biology at Ohio University and the lead researcher on the bobcat study told The Dispatch he did not yet have enough information to make that call. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;I would love to have a solid answer, but it&#39;s a tough question,&#34; Popescu told The Dispatch.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Public outcry by individuals like Cantino and groups like Save Ohio Bobcats II has persuaded the ODNRDOW to extend the &lt;a href=&#34;http://wildlife.ohiodnr.gov/stay-informed/proposed-rule-changes-csi-review/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;online commenting period&lt;/a&gt; until March 31 and push back the dates planned for the public hearing and vote. The Ohio Wildlife Council, a board of 8 that decides all ONDRDOW proposals, will hold a public meeting on April 11 and vote on May 9. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In her letter, Cantino pointed out that the council is composed entirely of sportspeople and does not have a biologist, though one member is a veterinarian. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;Only a massive outcry will defeat this dangerous, greedy, and ill-considered plan,&#34; she wrote.&lt;/p&gt;
                    
<div class="around-the-web">
    <div class="around-the-web__headline">Related Articles Around the Web</div>
    <ul class="around-the-web__list">
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">

        </li>
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="https://www.facebook.com/groups/1746762608715456/?ref=direct" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">Save Ohio&#39;s Bobcats - Open Group Public Group | Facebook &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
    </ul>
</div>

            <h1 class="widget__headline h1">
    <a class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/industrial-agriculture-water-pollution-2551396477.html" >
        From &#39;Sea to Shining Sea,&#39; Industrial Ag Fouls America&#39;s Waterways
    </a>
</h1>
        
    
        
            
            
        
    
        
            
            
<div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2551396477"
            
                data-authors="Common Dreams"
            
            
                data-headline="From &#39;Sea to Shining Sea,&#39; Industrial Ag Fouls America&#39;s Waterways"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief">
                <p>By Katherine Paul</p><p>A citizen-led group in Nebraska is <a href="https://www.organicconsumers.org/blog/costco-plans-fowl-project-nebraska" target="_blank">fighting</a> Costco's plan to build a huge chicken <a href="http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/factory-farming">factory farm</a> operation that residents in nearby cities say would pollute their <a href="http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/drinking-water">drinking water</a>.</p><p>Residents of Devils Lake, North Dakota, along with members of the Spirit Lake Nation Tribe are <a href="https://civileats.com/2018/03/20/putting-a-face-on-the-rural-fight-against-corporate-farms/" target="_blank">battling</a> plans to build a <a href="https://www.organicconsumers.org/essays/degeneration-nation-connecting-dots-between-factory-farms-roundup-gmos-and-fake-natural-foods" target="_blank">hog CAFO</a> in a neighboring community. They say the operation would pollute Devils Lake and area wetlands.</p>
            </div>
            <div class="js-appear-on-expand">
                <script type="keep-reading-content">
                        &lt;p&gt;On the east coast, industrial dairy operations have all but &lt;a href=&#34;http://regenerationvermont.org/wp-content/uploads/2015/12/RVT_VermontsGMOaddiction_9.pdf&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;ruined&lt;/a&gt; Vermont&#39;s lakes and rivers. One of the biggest offenders? Unilever-owned &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.organicconsumers.org/press/world-water-day-organic-consumers-assoc-renews-call-international-boycott-ben-jerrys&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Ben &amp;amp; Jerry&#39;s&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In North Carolina, industrial hog operations foul the state&#39;s waterways with &lt;a href=&#34;http://civileats.com/2016/06/28/north-carolinas-cafos-produce-15000-olympic-size-pools-worth-of-waste/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;10 billion gallons of feces and urine each year&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&#39;s no better on the west coast. In 2015, a federal judge &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.eenews.net/stories/1060011755&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;ruled&lt;/a&gt; that Washington&#39;s industrial dairy operations pose &#34;a significant public health risk by contaminating water supplies.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Whether it&#39;s the animal waste and &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/antibiotics&#34;&gt;antibiotics&lt;/a&gt; from livestock operations, or the nitrates and &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/pesticides&#34;&gt;pesticides&lt;/a&gt; from &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/gmo-genetically-modified-organism/&#34;&gt;GMO&lt;/a&gt; corn and soy grown to feed the millions of confined animals, from sea to shining sea, industrial &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/agribusiness&#34;&gt;agribusiness&lt;/a&gt;, led by multi-billion dollar corporations like &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.alternet.org/environment/tyson-foods-linked-largest-toxic-dead-zone-us-history&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Tyson&lt;/a&gt;, is destroying our most precious natural resource—&lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/water&#34;&gt;water&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;State and federal regulators by and large use their power to &lt;a href=&#34;http://civileats.com/2017/05/08/trump-executive-order-promises-to-slash-farm-regulations/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;protect&lt;/a&gt; corporate profits, not public health.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That leaves citizens to battle it out in the courts. But now some members of Congress want to eliminate even that last resort.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&#39;Nature for Water&#39;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thursday, March 22, is World Water Day. It&#39;s been 25 years since the United Nations designated the first annual international celebration of water.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This year&#39;s &lt;a href=&#34;http://foodtank710.acemlnc.com/lt.php?s=04ba17e907ab59e9f2e94b190d6b2662&amp;amp;i=197A274A1A51837&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;theme&lt;/a&gt;, &#34;Nature for Water,&#34; focuses on &lt;a href=&#34;http://foodtank710.acemlnc.com/lt.php?s=04ba17e907ab59e9f2e94b190d6b2662&amp;amp;i=197A274A1A51835&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;nature-based solutions&lt;/a&gt; for how to better manage the world&#39;s water.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We like it. Here&#39;s why.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to the UN, agriculture uses &lt;a href=&#34;http://worldwaterday.org/app/uploads/2018/02/fact_sheet_WWD2017_EN_2.pdf&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;70 percent&lt;/a&gt; of the world&#39;s available freshwater, including for crop irrigation and livestock production.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The UN also &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.fao.org/3/a-i7754e.pdf&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;reports&lt;/a&gt; that farms discharge large quantities of agrochemicals, organic matter, drug residues, sediments and saline drainage into water bodies, resulting in &#34;demonstrated risks to aquatic ecosystems, human health and productive activities.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;From the report:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=&#34;margin-left: 20px;&#34;&gt;Water pollution from agriculture has direct negative impacts on human health; for example, the well-known blue-baby syndrome in which high levels of nitrates in water can cause methaemoglobinemia—a potentially fatal illness—in infants. Pesticide accumulation in water and the food chain, with demonstrated ill effects on humans, led to the widespread banning of certain broad-spectrum and persistent pesticides (such as DDT and many organophosphates), but some such pesticides are still used in poorer countries, causing acute and likely chronic health effects. Aquatic ecosystems are also affected by agricultural pollution; for example, eutrophication caused by the accumulation of nutrients in lakes and coastal waters has impacts on biodiversity and fisheries.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The report also states that in the last 20 years, a new class of agricultural pollutants has emerged in the form of veterinary medicines (antibiotics, vaccines and growth promoters [hormones]), which move from farms through water to ecosystems and drinking-water sources.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The upshot? Agriculture is the biggest user, and one of the biggest polluters, of the world&#39;s water.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;No Help From the Feds&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Given agriculture&#39;s role in destroying our most precious natural resource, you&#39;d think the U.S. Congress would do everything in its power to protect your water.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Instead, it&#39;s moving in the opposite direction.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In 2015, Congress passed the &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/energy-environment/wp/2017/02/20/trump-to-roll-back-obamas-climate-water-rules-through-executive-action/?utm_term=.4453480d771d&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Waters of the United States&lt;/a&gt; (WOTUS) rule, in an attempt to bring more clarity to the Clean Water Act. Specifically, the rule was intended to protect U.S. waterways from polluters, including agricultural operations, which have historically enjoyed a high level of &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.organicconsumers.org/essays/who-should-clean-big-ag%E2%80%99s-mess&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;exemptions&lt;/a&gt; under the Clean Water Act.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Enter the Trump administration, which has asked the U.S. &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/epa&#34;&gt;Environmental Protection Agency&lt;/a&gt; and the Army Corps of Engineers to &#34;revisit&#34; WOTUS, which according to the &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/energy-environment/wp/2017/02/27/trump-to-direct-rollback-of-obama-era-water-rule-tuesday/?utm_term=.9895df0e7ad3&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Washington Post&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/trump-watch/&#34;&gt;Trump&lt;/a&gt; described as &#34;paving the way for the elimination of this very destructive and horrible rule.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So much for regulatory relief.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But even before Trump attacked WOTUS, some members of Congress began looking for additional ways to protect agribusiness profits—by making it impossible for citizens to sue companies that destroy their drinking water.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Last year, Rep. Dan Newhouse (R-Wash.) introduced a bill (&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.congress.gov/bill/115th-congress/house-bill/848&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;H.R. 848&lt;/a&gt;, the Farm Regulatory Certainty Act), to strip citizens&#39; ability to file lawsuits under the Resource Conservation and Recovery Act (RCRA) against factory farms that have polluted their drinking water. If passed, the bill would leave rural Americans with no recourse if their drinking water is polluted by mismanagement of livestock waste on a factory farm. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No surprise that Newhouse would lead this charge. It was in his state that citizens successfully used &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.perkinscoie.com/en/news-insights/ruling-applies-rcra-to-dairy-practices-for-the-first-time.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;RCRA&lt;/a&gt; to go after factory farm dairies that had made their water too polluted to even bathe in, much less drink. After a 15-year struggle, the courts finally forced companies to clean up their acts, and also provide hundreds of families with drinking water until their wells could be restored.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If Newhouse and his co-sponsors in the U.S. House get their way, dairy farm manure would be exempt from any regulations, and lawsuits like the one filed by Washington state citizens would be &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.yakimaherald.com/news/local/newhouse-bill-would-exempt-dairy-manure-from-waste-regulations-ban/article_7198943a-19c0-11e7-a09a-ffb04764f295.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;banned&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;What Can You Do?&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Back to World Water Day.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We like the &#34;nature-based&#34; solutions for cleaning up the world&#39;s water because it leads us away from the dominant corporate agribusiness model, to the &lt;a href=&#34;http://regenerationinternational.org/why-regenerative-agriculture/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;regenerative&lt;/a&gt; organic model—a model that works &lt;em&gt;with&lt;/em&gt; nature to build healthy soil that retains more water, and uses practices that don&#39;t include synthetic chemicals and a host of dangerous chemicals like &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.organicconsumers.org/news/glyphosate-fact-sheet&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;glyphosate&lt;/a&gt;, 2,4-D, atrazine, &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.organicconsumers.org/news/us-epas-chlorpyrifos-decision-spurs-pushback&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;chlorpyrifos&lt;/a&gt; and others.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The corporate industrial ag empire will tell us it can&#39;t be done. But as consumers, concerned citizens, ethical business owners, independent farmers and countless others will ultimately prove, it can.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What can you do? To help speed the transition to an agriculture model that heals our waterways, rather than exploit and pollute them?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here are a few concrete steps you can take.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul class=&#34;ee-ul&#34;&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Get involved in the Regeneration Movement. &lt;/strong&gt;You can start by &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.regenerationinternational.org/newsletter&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;signing up&lt;/a&gt; for the Regeneration International newsletter, and by learning more about how to join or build a local or regional &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.regenerationinternational.org/regenerative-alliances/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Regeneration Alliance&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;ul class=&#34;ee-ul&#34;&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Tell Congress to protect your water! &lt;/strong&gt;Call your members of Congress with these two asks: 1) Ask them to protect WOTUS which would at least help hold more companies accountable for ag-related water pollution; 2) Ask them to protect citizens&#39; right to clean drinking water by rejecting Rep. Newhouse&#39;s &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.yakimaherald.com/news/local/newhouse-bill-would-exempt-dairy-manure-from-waste-regulations-ban/article_7198943a-19c0-11e7-a09a-ffb04764f295.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;H.R. 848&lt;/a&gt;. Find your Representatives&#39; phone numbers &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.house.gov/representatives/find-your-representative&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;here&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;ul class=&#34;ee-ul&#34;&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Ask Congress to support Rep. Earl Blumenauer&#39;s (D-Ore.) Food &amp;amp; Farm Act. &lt;/strong&gt;This is the year Congress rewrites the Farm Bill. Historically, the Farm Bill has doled out millions in subsidies to corporations like Monsanto, Tyson, Cargill, Dow—big water polluters—while ignoring the farmers who produce healthy food, using practices that build healthy soil. Please &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.house.gov/representatives/find-your-representative&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;use our alert&lt;/a&gt; to contact your members of Congress and ask them to support the Food &amp;amp; Farm Act.&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;ul class=&#34;ee-ul&#34;&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Buy organic and regenerative! &lt;/strong&gt;Consumers are key to successfully shifting our country&#39;s food and farming system from one that degenerates to one that regenerates. The more we use our consumer dollars to support the farmers and producers who understand the importance of regenerating our soil and water, the more likely they will succeed.&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;
        &lt;p class=&#34;shortcode-media shortcode-media-twitter_embed&#34;&gt;
        &lt;iframe class=&#34;rm-shortcode&#34; data-rm-shortcode-id=&#34;RC26001521747314&#34; frameborder=&#34;0&#34; height=&#34;150&#34; id=&#34;twitter-embed-949948766123110400&#34; scrolling=&#34;no&#34; src=&#34;/res/community/twitter_embed/?iframe_id=twitter-embed-949948766123110400&amp;amp;created_ts=1515320409.0&amp;amp;screen_name=EcoWatch&amp;amp;text=Let%27s+Make+2018+the+Year+We+Rise+Up+and+Regenerate%21+https%3A%2F%2Ft.co%2FENeJv7mmJQ+%40GreenpeaceUK+%40GreenpeaceAustP+%40globalactplan&amp;amp;id=949948766123110400&amp;amp;name=EcoWatch&#34; width=&#34;100%&#34;&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;
        &lt;/p&gt;

        </li>
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="https://www.ucsusa.org/food_and_agriculture/our-failing-food-system/industrial-agriculture/hidden-costs-of-industrial.html" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">Hidden Costs of Industrial Agriculture | Union of Concerned Scientists &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
    </ul>
</div>

                </script>
            </div>
            <div class="widget__show-more with-primary-color js-keep-reading js-page-view-and-reload-embeds">
                <span class="show-more js-expand">Keep reading...</span>

    <span class="social-date__text">7h</span>

    </div><div class="widget__head">

                    
                    

                        

                        
                        
                        

                        <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/great-pacific-garbage-patch-texas-2551330463.html" >
                            
                            <div class="widget__image crop-2x1"
                                
                                    
                                    data-runner-img-hd="https://assets.rbl.ms/17462826/1200x600.jpg"
                                    data-runner-img-md="https://assets.rbl.ms/17462826/600x300.jpg"

        Plastic samples collected from the Great Pacific garbage patch. <em>The Ocean Cleanup</em>

            <h1 class="widget__headline h1">
    <a class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/great-pacific-garbage-patch-texas-2551330463.html" >
        Great Pacific Garbage Patch Is Now Twice the Size of Texas
    </a>
</h1>
        
    
        
            
            
        
    
        
            
            
<div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2551330463"
            
                data-authors="Lorraine Chow"
            
            
                data-headline="Great Pacific Garbage Patch Is Now Twice the Size of Texas"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief">
                <p>
	The 
	<a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/plastic-ocean-cleanup-boyan-slat-2028954014.html" target="_blank">Great Pacific Garbage Patch</a> (GPGP) floating off the coast of California now measures 1.6 million square kilometers (about 1 million square miles), according to a startling new study. To put that into perspective, the clump of trash is about the size of three Frances, or twice the size of Texas.
</p><p>
	Not only that, the 
	<a href="https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-018-22939-w" target="_blank">analysis</a>, published Thursday in the journal Scientific Reports, also revealed that the massive Pacific trash vortex contains up to 16 times more <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/plastics" target="_blank">plastic</a> than previous estimates—<a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/oceans-plastic-pollution-2550762861.html" target="_blank">and could rapidly get worse</a>.
</p>
            </div>
            <div class="js-appear-on-expand">
                <script type="keep-reading-content">
                        &lt;p&gt;
	The researchers estimated there are about 1.8 trillion pieces of plastic weighing 80,000 metric tons,  the equivalent of 500 Jumbo Jets, are currently afloat in the area. That&#39;s largest accumulation zone for 
	&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/oceans&#34;&gt;ocean&lt;/a&gt; plastics on Earth.
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&#34;shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image&#34;&gt;
	&lt;img class=&#34;rm-shortcode&#34; data-rm-shortcode-id=&#34;SZFIGZ1521745381&#34; data-rm-shortcode-name=&#34;rebelmouse-image&#34; id=&#34;f3467&#34; src=&#34;https://assets.rbl.ms/17462005/980x.jpg&#34;/&gt;
	&lt;small class=&#34;image-media media-caption&#34; placeholder=&#34;add caption...&#34;&gt;The Great Pacific Garbage Patch contains up to 16 times more plastic than previously estimated.&lt;/small&gt;&lt;small class=&#34;image-media media-photo-credit&#34; placeholder=&#34;add photo credit...&#34;&gt;The Ocean Cleanup&lt;/small&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	The study is the result of a three-year mapping effort conducted by an international team of scientists affiliated with Dutch non-profit 
	&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.theoceancleanup.com/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;The Ocean Cleanup Foundation&lt;/a&gt;, six universities and an aerial sensor company.
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	According to a press release provided to EcoWatch, to analyze the full extent of the GPGP, the team conducted a comprehensive sampling effort by crossing the debris field with 30 vessels simultaneously, supplemented by two aircraft surveys. The fleet collected a total of 1.2 million plastic samples, while the aerial sensors scanned more than 300 square kilometers of ocean surface.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&#34;shortcode-media shortcode-media-rebelmouse-image&#34;&gt;
        &lt;img class=&#34;rm-shortcode&#34; data-rm-shortcode-id=&#34;KVOEKY1521745381&#34; data-rm-shortcode-name=&#34;rebelmouse-image&#34; id=&#34;6aee0&#34; src=&#34;https://assets.rbl.ms/17462800/980x.jpg&#34;/&gt;
        &lt;small class=&#34;image-media media-caption&#34; placeholder=&#34;add caption...&#34;&gt; About 1.8 trillion pieces of plastic weighing 80,000 metric tons are currently afloat in the area. &lt;/small&gt;&lt;small class=&#34;image-media media-photo-credit&#34; placeholder=&#34;add photo credit...&#34;&gt;The Ocean Cleanup&lt;/small&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The results showed that 92 percent of the mass was represented by larger objects—such as 
	&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/ghost-gear-oceans-2544526011.html&#34;&gt;discarded fishing nets&lt;/a&gt; several meters in size—and 8 percent consisted of &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/microplastics&#34;&gt;microplastics&lt;/a&gt; smaller than 5 millimeters in size.&lt;br/&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	&#34;We were surprised by the amount of large plastic objects we encountered,&#34; said Dr. Julia Reisser, chief scientist of the expeditions in a statement. &#34;We used to think most of the debris consists of small fragments, but this new analysis shines a new light on the scope of the debris.&#34;
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	The team found that plastic pollution levels within the garbage patch have grown exponentially since measurements began in the 1970s.
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&#34;shortcode-media shortcode-media-twitter_embed&#34;&gt;
	&lt;iframe class=&#34;rm-shortcode&#34; data-rm-shortcode-id=&#34;MJAA571521745305&#34; frameborder=&#34;0&#34; height=&#34;150&#34; id=&#34;twitter-embed-976469248297095168&#34; scrolling=&#34;no&#34; src=&#34;/res/community/twitter_embed/?iframe_id=twitter-embed-976469248297095168&amp;amp;created_ts=1521643385.0&amp;amp;screen_name=EcoWatch&amp;amp;text=Ocean+Plastic+Projected+to+Triple+Within+Seven+Years+https%3A%2F%2Ft.co%2FvlK2Wm6myn+%40Oceana+%40PlasticPollutes+%40WWF%E2%80%A6+https%3A%2F%2Ft.co%2FcgxX2WZufn&amp;amp;id=976469248297095168&amp;amp;name=EcoWatch&#34; width=&#34;100%&#34;&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	&#34;This plastic accumulation rate inside the GPGP, which was greater than in the surrounding waters, indicates that the inflow of plastic into the patch continues to exceed the outflow,&#34; said Laurent Lebreton, lead author of the study.
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	The Ocean Cleanup team is preparing to launch its highly anticipated 
	&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.theoceancleanup.com/technology/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;cleanup system&lt;/a&gt; in the Great Pacific Garbage Patch this summer with the goal of collecting 50 percent of the trash in five years.
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	&#34;To be able to solve a problem, we believe it is essential to first understand it,&#34; said 
	&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/tag/boyan-slat&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Boyan Slat&lt;/a&gt;, founder of The Ocean Cleanup and co-author of the study. &#34;These results provide us with key data to develop and test our cleanup technology, but it also underlines the urgency of dealing with the plastic pollution problem. Since the results indicate that the amount of hazardous microplastics is set to increase more than tenfold if left to fragment, the time to start is now.&#34;
&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;
	Slat, who 
	&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.ecowatch.com/boyan-slat-to-deploy-longest-floating-structure-in-world-history-to-cl-1882045844.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;shot to fame&lt;/a&gt; five years ago with claims that his invention could clean up the seas, explains the methodology and results of the new study in the video below:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p class=&#34;shortcode-media shortcode-media-youtube&#34;&gt;
        &lt;iframe class=&#34;rm-shortcode&#34; data-rm-shortcode-id=&#34;AQUM7V1521745381&#34; frameborder=&#34;0&#34; height=&#34;480&#34; scrolling=&#34;no&#34; src=&#34;https://www.youtube.com/embed/VxMATP5oRx4?rel=0&#34; width=&#34;100%&#34;&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;
        &lt;/p&gt;
                    
<div class="around-the-web">
    <div class="around-the-web__headline">Related Articles Around the Web</div>
    <ul class="around-the-web__list">
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/adidas-shoes-ocean-plastic-2547494872.html" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">Adidas Sold a Million Shoes Made of Ocean Plastic &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="https://www.ecowatch.com/oceans-plastic-pollution-2550762861.html" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">Ocean Plastic Projected to Triple Within Seven Years &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
        <li class="around-the-web__item">
            <a href="https://www.theoceancleanup.com/" class="around-the-web__link" target="_blank">The Ocean Cleanup &rsaquo;</a>
        </li>
        
    </ul>
</div>

                </script>

            <h1 class="widget__headline h1">
    <a class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/deep-sea-mining-minerals-2551301225.html" >
        Deep Sea Mining Decisions: Approaching the Point of No Return
    </a>
</h1>
        
    
        
            
            
        
    
        
            
            
<div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2551301225"
            
                data-authors="Greenpeace"
            
            
                data-headline="Deep Sea Mining Decisions: Approaching the Point of No Return"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief">
                <p> By Sebastian Losada</p><p>Over the last two weeks, the <a href="https://www.isa.org.jm/" target="_blank">International Seabed Authority (ISA)</a> has been in discussions in Jamaica. Its mission—to work towards the finalization of exploitation regulations, a so-called <a href="https://mercury.postlight.com/amp?url=https://www.newsdeeply.com/oceans/articles/2017/09/06/seabed-mining-the-24-people-who-could-decide-the-fate-of-the-deep-ocean" target="_blank">mining code</a>, that will allow commercial deep sea mining operations to begin all around the world. </p><p>In the quest for minerals, deep seabed mining means to extend mining activities into the deep ocean. The coming two years are critical in the opening—or not—of this unnecessary new frontier of resource exploitation. </p>
            </div>
            <div class="js-appear-on-expand">
                <script type="keep-reading-content">
                        &lt;p&gt;The deep sea covers around fifty percent of the earth&#39;s &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/oceans&#34;&gt;oceans&lt;/a&gt; and a great share of that is in international waters. Hidden under thousands of meters of water, the vast majority of it hasn&#39;t been explored, meaning deep seabed mining could &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.frontiersin.org/articles/10.3389/fmars.2018.00053/full&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;wipe out species&lt;/a&gt; and ecosystems before we even know them. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The ISA regulates the sea floor outside nations&#39; jurisdiction. It has to decide what the rules are: how much money will go to developing countries and their communities, what kind of environmental controls there will be. And, right now, decisions that could impact the earth&#39;s seabed forever are being made in Jamaica, ushered through under pressure from industry and mining advocates with a decided lack of transparency. &lt;strong&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Advocates of ocean exploitation, like &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.lockheedmartin.com/uk/news/press-releases/2013-press-releases/uk-government-sponsors-lockheed-martin-uk-subsidiary-for-licence.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;U.S. giant military company Lockheed Martin&lt;/a&gt;, argue that we need deep sea mining in order to meet &#34;the growing global demand for precious metals&#34; and to support &#34;economic growth.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;They also claim that deep sea mining is necessary to satisfy our endless thirst for technological and electronic innovation. This conveniently ignores many aspects of the problem.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&#34;Are we going to continue to develop huge mines that destroy villages, alter rivers, pollute water courses, take thousands of years to restore, remove whole mountains? You don&#39;t have any of that with deep seabed mining,&#34; said ISA Secretary General Michael Lodge, &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/resources/idt-sh/deep_sea_mining&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;recently.&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While it&#39;s true that mining for essential and finite raw materials often endangers workers and leaves the earth irreversibly scarred, the solution is not—and cannot be—to translate these mining impacts to &lt;em&gt;other&lt;/em&gt; ecosystems that provide crucial services to humanity and our climate. Doing so would not only result in potentially irreversible &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/biodiversity&#34;&gt;biodiversity&lt;/a&gt; losses, but would also send a completely wrong signal: that we do not need to improve efficiency and reduce resource use because there is plenty down there.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Why is it that the IT sector, and its current leaders such as Samsung and Apple, can show the ingenuity to develop technologies that allow us to do things we could only have dreamed of a decade ago, but do not put such ingenuity to the service of a truly sustainable economy within the boundaries of the planet? &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Instead, in the race to gain market share, IT companies increasingly change the design of their products in a way that accelerates the replacement cycle, making &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.greenpeace.org/usa/reports/greener-electronics-2017/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;them difficult to service, upgrade or repair and shortening their useful life&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Mining advocates also argue that we need the minerals to meet increased demand from the growth of renewable energy technologies and the electrification of transport.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But there is no evidence that a transition towards renewable energy necessitates mining in the deep ocean. On the contrary; a recent &lt;a href=&#34;http://dscc.hifrontier.com/wp-content/uploads/2017/03/Teske_Sven_ISF-Kingston-11-July-2016.pdf&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;report&lt;/a&gt; by the Institute for Sustainable Futures found that: &#34;Even with the projected very high demand growth rates under the most ambitious energy scenarios, the projected increase in cumulative demand—all within the range of known terrestrial resources—does not require deep-sea mining activity.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Different types of seabed mining involve different extraction methods and technologies, but whatever the approach, severe impacts can be expected. Sediment plumes, the potential release of toxic chemicals, habitat destruction, increased temperature and noise all threaten the deep sea&#39;s precious and as yet untouched environment. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Researchers recently concluded that &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.nature.com/articles/ngeo2983&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;most mining-induced loss of biodiversity&lt;/a&gt; in the deep sea is likely to last forever on human timescales, given the very slow natural rates of recovery in affected ecosystems. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet the ISA has recently rejected the establishment of an environmental committee to better include environmental considerations in its functioning, and key environmental information is not public. Its Legal and Technical Commission meets mostly behind closed doors, and its composition is such that biological and ecological considerations are underrepresented. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite all the arguments against this unnecessary pillaging of our planet&#39;s seabed, so far the ISA has approved &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.isa.org.jm/deep-seabed-minerals-contractors&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;28 exploration contracts&lt;/a&gt; in the Pacific, Indian, and Atlantic oceans—covering more than 1.4 million square kilometers (approximately 540,000 square miles), roughly &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.nationmaster.com/country-info/stats/Geography/Land-area/Sq.-km&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;four times&lt;/a&gt; the size of Germany—to companies like Lockheed Martin. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And in the meantime, the first commercial test case for the deep seabed mining industry is already planned to take place in the waters of Papua New Guinea. Canadian company &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.nautilusminerals.com/irm/content/default.aspx&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Nautilus Minerals&lt;/a&gt; plans to extract mineral-rich sulfides, containing copper, zinc and gold, at depths between 1,500 and 2,000m. The mining operation, known as the Solwara project, is scheduled to begin early in 2019.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A strong &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.deepseaminingoutofourdepth.org/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;alliance&lt;/a&gt; of NGOs made of more than 20 communities in the &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.facebook.com/bismarckramugroup/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Bismarck&lt;/a&gt; and Solomon Seas is &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.facebook.com/Alliance-of-Solwara-Warriors-234267050262483/about/?ref=page_internal&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;fighting to stop the project&lt;/a&gt;. Arguing a &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.looppng.com/png-news/alliance-solwara-warriors-lodge-objection-73970&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;lack of consultation&lt;/a&gt;, and drawing attention to the grave impacts that could be derived from the project, the local opposition is growing stronger while the company is &lt;a href=&#34;http://dsmobserver.org/2018/01/challenges-at-nautilus/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;facing potential financial troubles. &lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The European Union Parliament has recently agreed on a &lt;a href=&#34;http://www.europarl.europa.eu/sides/getDoc.do?pubRef=-//EP//TEXT+TA+P8-TA-2018-0004+0+DOC+XML+V0//EN&amp;amp;language=EN&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;resolution on international oceans governance&lt;/a&gt;, which calls for a moratorium on seabed mining. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While opposition is mounting, alternative economic models are gaining momentum and people are increasingly aware of what&#39;s at stake. For the sake of the oceans, the planet and the people, it&#39;s urgent that we stand to prevent commercial deep sea mining before it&#39;s too late.&lt;/p&gt;
        &lt;p class=&#34;shortcode-media shortcode-media-twitter_embed&#34;&gt;
        &lt;iframe class=&#34;rm-shortcode&#34; data-rm-shortcode-id=&#34;9ZEFN11521751028&#34; frameborder=&#34;0&#34; height=&#34;150&#34; id=&#34;twitter-embed-763976732961443841&#34; scrolling=&#34;no&#34; src=&#34;/res/community/twitter_embed/?iframe_id=twitter-embed-763976732961443841&amp;amp;created_ts=1470981221.0&amp;amp;screen_name=iamgreenbean&amp;amp;text=Human+Consumption+of+Earth%27s+Natural+Resources+Has+Tripled+in+40+Years+via+%40EcoWatch+https%3A%2F%2Ft.co%2Ffi5IO7hF8P+%23deforestation+%23biodiversity&amp;amp;id=763976732961443841&amp;amp;name=Green+Bean&#34; width=&#34;100%&#34;&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;
        &lt;/p&gt;
        &lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;em&gt;Sebastian Losada is the oceans policy adviser for Greenpeace International.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;

            <h1 class="widget__headline h1">
    <a class="widget__headline-text custom-post-headline" href="https://www.ecowatch.com/save-the-swimmers-2550805366.html" >
        One Mom’s Campaign to Save the Swimmers
    </a>
</h1>
        
    
        
            
            
        
    
        
            
            
<div class="body js-expandable clearfix js-listicle-body js-update-url"
        
            data-elid="2550805366"
            
                data-authors="U.S. Right to Know"
            
            
                data-headline="One Mom’s Campaign to Save the Swimmers"
            
        >

        <div class="body-description"><div class="widget__brief">
                <p>By Stacy Malkan</p><p>"I want grandkids one day, so sperm is important to me because I've got three young boys," said mom, author and social media genius Leah Segedie in a <a href="https://www.mamavation.com/savetheswimmers" target="_blank">video</a> introducing her "Save the Swimmers" campaign.</p><p>"This is where my youngest rolls his eyes at me and says, 'I know Mom. Avoiding <a href="http://www.ecowatch.com/tag/plastics">plastics</a> can help save my swimmers, oooh kay.' But to me this is no laughing matter. Over 25 years of studies have demonstrated that these little sperm are crying out for help."</p>
            </div>
            <div class="js-appear-on-expand">
                <script type="keep-reading-content">
                        &lt;p&gt;In her new book, out now from Rodale Books, Segedie explains what the science is saying about the impact of hormone-disrupting chemicals on male fertility. As she puts it, she drops science, along with truth bombs about how the government is failing to ensure the safety of toxic-chemical-laden products and food, and many practical tips about how to protect our families without going bonkers. The book is called &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.rodalebooks.com/green-enough/&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;&lt;em&gt;Green Enough: Eat Better, Live Cleaner, Be Happier (All Without Driving Your Family Crazy)&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As the founder of &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.mamavation.com&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;Mamavation.com&lt;/a&gt;, a mom-focused social media community devoted to practical conversations about healthy living, Segedie doesn&#39;t let eye rolls stop her from delivering her message in the most straightforward and relatable way she can, cuss words and all. One headline in the book describes the food safety system in the U.S. as, &#34;Generally Recognized as &lt;del&gt;Safe&lt;/del&gt; Bullshit.&#34;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what do Moms need to know about toxic chemicals in our homes and how to protect our loved ones and their swimmers? I asked Leah Segedie to share some of the takeaway messages from her book. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Stacy Malkan: You spent a lot of time studying up on endocrine disrupting chemicals (EDCs)—chemicals that can mimic or block our body&#39;s natural hormones—and one of the pioneering scientists in the field, Pete Myers, was your science advisor for your book. Tell us what the research on EDCs is saying. &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Leah Segedie: &lt;/strong&gt;The most important part of what I&#39;ve learned is simple—dose doesn&#39;t equal the poison when it comes to hormone disrupting chemicals. Smaller amounts can have a more detrimental impact on pregnant women and children than larger ones. And chemicals do unpredictable things at low doses, which has been demonstrated in tons of studies. The scariest part is how regulators ignore low-dose effects. Lots of products contain low doses of these hormone-disrupting chemicals. In &lt;em&gt;Green Enough&lt;/em&gt; I tell you exactly what those chemicals are and how you can avoid most of them. The most vulnerable of the population are pregnant women and small children. But those aren&#39;t the only people at risk. Hormone disruption is more likely to happen when you are going through a hormonal change, so in addition to considering this for pregnant women and children, the same is true for adolescents going through puberty and women going through menopause. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And then there is the toxic soup environment to consider. We don&#39;t know the impact of what happens when different chemicals mix together in our bodies. So with the 85,000 chemicals in commerce, we actually have no idea what they are doing and how they are impacting our health. Long story short, we are not protected the way everyone thinks. Chemicals are evaluated based on old and outdated technology and techniques. And we need to update how we treat and evaluate chemicals in this country. But I say don&#39;t wait for them to get their act together. It&#39;s your life and your family—take control of your own home environment. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Malkan&lt;/em&gt;: What are your top tips for saving the swimmers? &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Segedie:&lt;/strong&gt; When I talk about the Save the Swimmers campaign in my home, I&#39;m referring to how I got my husband and young boys on board with all the changes I needed to make. And it was simple. I explained to them what is happening to &lt;a href=&#34;https://www.nytimes.com/2017/03/11/opinion/sunday/are-your-sperm-in-trouble.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;sperm health&lt;/a&gt; in the United States. About 90 percent of young men have some degree of unhealthy sperm. Did they want unhealthy sperm that could prevent them from having children one day? Well then, we need to make some change in this house to dumb down the amount of hormone disrupting chemicals we have everywhere. I involved them in the process and was clear why it was happening. It was about as simple as that. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;My top tips for avoiding hormone-disrupting chemicals: &lt;/p&gt; &lt;p style=&#34;margin-left: 20px;&#34;&gt;1. Stop eating or drinking from plastic.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=&#34;margin-left: 20px;&#34;&gt;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;2. Stop using synthetic fragrances in the home, especially ones that don&#39;t list their ingredients.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=&#34;margin-left: 20px;&#34;&gt;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;3. Pay attention to what&#39;s in your personal care products.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=&#34;margin-left: 20px;&#34;&gt;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;4, Notice what your food is wrapped in.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=&#34;margin-left: 20px;&#34;&gt;&lt;span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;5. When you make changes, make them slowly so they stick and are accepted by your family. When things happen to quickly, it creates a mutiny.&lt;/p&gt; &lt;p&gt;I&#39;d say that starting with food would be the most important part, not just what ingredients you chose, but also the chemicals coming from packaging, cookware, storage containers and cans. Getting a handle on these &#34;indirect additives&#34; is the quickest way to get toxic chemicals out of the food you eat. In &lt;em&gt;Green Enough&lt;/em&gt;, I discuss how to purge your kitchen of these chemicals, what to do when you find them and what products are safer solutions.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Malkan&lt;/em&gt;: What has been the biggest challenge in getting your family on board? &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Segedie: &lt;/strong&gt;I&#39;m very honest with my family about why I&#39;m doing things and I involve them in those decisions all the time. In the beginning, I sat them down and explained I wanted to have grandkids one day and there were chemicals inside our home that have the potential to prevent that from happening. And I needed their help to ensure they had healthy sperm. It seems that boys care a great deal about how virile they are, even when they are young, so that worked to my advantage. So when I have to make adjustments, I tell them &#34;I want grandkids one day ... do you want kids? Okay then do you agree this is wise?&#34; and I get a yes and possibly an eye roll. But that&#39;s really all I need. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;My biggest challenges are not my two youngest but my oldest son and my husband. My husband is more set in his ways and we aren&#39;t planning on having more children so he joked with me for a time that he could do whatever he wanted and his sperm could become green with mutation and it would be fine. My oldest son is just starting go through puberty so all he wants to do is things his friends are doing. But he&#39;s very much a thinker and prides himself on being smart so I blow up his ego by saying he knows more about endocrine disruption than most adults and he gets a kick out of that part. For those two I just have to reward them more. The younger two are fine with me giving them a hug and me telling them how proud I am of them. But you&#39;d be surprised how far they got in a couple of years ... even with eye rolling. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;em&gt;Malkan&lt;/em&gt;: A recent study found high levels of endocrine disrupting chemicals called &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.nytimes.com/2017/07/12/well/eat/the-chemicals-in-your-mac-and-cheese.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;&lt;em&gt;phthalates in macaroni and cheese&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;. As the mom of a toddler who loves mac and cheese, this one is a real challenge. What&#39;s your &#39;green enough&#39; guidance? &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Segedie: &lt;/strong&gt;Phthalates (said &#34;Thal-8s&#34;) are in all dairy products at varying levels, including organic dairy. But the rule about dairy is simple: the more processing the dairy goes through the higher the phthalate level. And this is why powdered cheese has the highest level of phthalate contamination. We still enjoy dairy in my home, so I find other places to lessen the amount we are exposed to. I end up purchasing low fat versions of yogurt and string cheese because it&#39;s hiding inside the fat. I know fat is all the rage right now, but fat from animal products is also a very efficient delivery method for these chemicals. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As for the solution to your mac and cheese problem, I would recommend instead of using the powdered cheese, make your own mac and cheese with milk and cheese you grated yourself. That will cut down on the phthalate level instantly. And if you are really worried about phthalate contamination, start paying more attention to other places where phthalates are hiding. The final two chapters of &lt;em&gt;Green Enough&lt;/em&gt; will help you lessen phthalate contamination inside each room of your home and your personal care products. These complicated scenarios were a primary focus of the book. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Malkan&lt;/em&gt;: The green path can be a lonely one. What&#39;s your best advice for having the green enough conversation with reluctant loved ones? &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Segedie: &lt;/strong&gt;I would say that if they don&#39;t live inside your home don&#39;t lecture them. Just live your life the way you need to and eventually they will ask you why you do things the way you do and that is your opportunity to tell them. Wait until they ask you. That&#39;s when they are more likely to accept what you have to say. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Malkan&lt;/em&gt;: You have been sparking discussions on social media about the role of women in the food industry. Do you think the natural products industry has a gender inequity problem?&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Segedie: &lt;/strong&gt;Yes, I do believe the natural industry could use more women and people of color in charge. It&#39;s ironic that women are their primary customers and yet decisions that impact them don&#39;t involve them. And it&#39;s typical of industries that are growing and seeing a bull market, so to speak. Not only would I like to see more female CEOs and people of color, but I&#39;d also like to see them sitting on the boards of directors. For an industry that prides itself on being &#34;better,&#34; in this case they really are not. &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=&#34;https://www.mamavation.com/2018/03/the-natural-industry-needs-more-women-leaders.html&#34; target=&#34;_blank&#34;&gt;I wrote about this the other day after having a very unpleasant experience&lt;/a&gt; with someone very powerful in the industry. And I think the solution is really simple: as women we need to start using our voices more and supporting brands that respect us and give us a seat at the table. Now there are so many organic brands to choose from, we can start getting picky. I&#39;m starting to create a list of these brands and I encourage other women to start sharing their stories to create more awareness around this issue. We deserve this!&lt;/p&gt;
